Let's analyze more systematically.

We need to find the outcome for each λ. Let's denote the game state. Possibly the game can be analyzed by considering the "potential" P_n = S_n^2 - 2Q_n or something. Another approach: This game is similar to a "DDR" type where each player controls a different resource. It might be solved by finding a "critical" λ such that Alice can force Q_n > n+1 at some even n. We can try to derive necessary and sufficient conditions.

Define for each odd n, after Alice's move, we have S_n ≤ λ n. For each even n, after Bazza's move, Q_n ≤ n. The players choose x_n to maintain these constraints. The game continues as long as both constraints hold. The game ends if at some turn the player cannot choose a nonnegative x_n satisfying the inequality. The player who cannot move loses.

We can think of the game as a "battle" between the sum and sum of squares. The players can try to force the other's cumulative sum or cumulative square sum to exceed the bound.

We need to determine for each λ, which player has a winning strategy. We suspect there is a threshold λ* such that for λ > λ*, Alice wins; for λ < λ*, Bazza wins; and perhaps λ = λ* is a draw? Let's analyze the general strategy: For Alice, she wants to force Q_n > n+1 at some even n. She can try to always choose the maximum allowed on her turns. Similarly, Bazza might choose the maximum allowed to try to cause S to exceed λ n later. But if Alice always chooses max, we can compute the evolution of S and Q. So if Alice always chooses max, then Bazza's best response is also max. So the outcome is determined by the asymptotic behavior of the process when both play max. So we need to analyze the recurrence.

Let's define for each odd n, after Alice's move, S_n = λ n (since she chooses max). For even n, after Bazza's move, Q_n = n (since he chooses max). But is that consistent? In the simulation for λ=0.9, we had Q2=2.0, Q4≈4.0, Q6≈6.0, Q8≈8.0, so indeed Q_{2k} = 2k. So it seems that if both play max, then at even steps, Q_{2k} = 2k, and at odd steps, S_{2k+1} = λ (2k+1). Let's check the recurrence.

At odd step n=1: Alice chooses x1 = λ, so S1=λ, Q1=λ^2.
At even step n=2: Bazza chooses x2 = sqrt(2 - Q1) = sqrt(2 - λ^2). Then S2 = λ + sqrt(2-λ^2). Q2 = λ^2 + (2-λ^2)=2.
At odd step n=3: Alice chooses x3 = 3λ - S2 = 3λ - (λ + sqrt(2-λ^2)) = 2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2). Then S3 = 3λ. Q3 = Q2 + x3^2 = 2 + (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2.
At even step n=4: Bazza chooses x4 = sqrt(4 - Q3) = sqrt(4 - [2 + (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2]) = sqrt(2 - (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2). Then S4 = S3 + x4 = 3λ + sqrt(2 - (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2). Q4 = Q3 + x4^2 = 2 + (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2 + [2 - (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2] = 4.
So indeed, Q4 = 4. So the pattern: At even steps, Q_{2k} = 2k, and at odd steps, S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). This can be proved by induction. The key is that the recurrence for Q_{2k} yields exactly 2k because the squares add up to the bound. So if both play max, the game continues indefinitely with Q_{2k}=2k and S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1). Then Alice's sum bound is always satisfied exactly at her turns, and Bazza's square bound is exactly satisfied at his turns. So the game goes on forever, and neither wins, provided that the choices are always possible. Thus the process when both play max is determined by the sequence of states. Let's derive the recurrence for the maximum possible Q_n and S_n.

We can consider the game as a "resource" game where each player tries to maximize the other's cumulative sum. The outcome might be determined by a "critical" λ such that the process of "max" leads to a "win" for one player.

We can try to analyze the game by considering the "potential" function F_n = S_n^2 - 2Q_n. Compute its evolution. At odd turn n, Alice chooses x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (max). Then S_n = λ n. Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2. So F_n = (λ n)^2 - 2(Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2). At even turn n, Bazza chooses x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) (max). Then Q_n = n. S_n = S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). So F_n = S_n^2 - 2n.

We can compute the evolution of F. But maybe we can find an invariant.

Alternatively, note that the constraints are linear and quadratic. The game is similar to a "game of sums" where players have opposing objectives. A common technique is to consider that each player can adopt a "safe" strategy that ensures the game goes on forever, and then find the threshold where one player can force a win. For λ small, Bazza can force a win; for λ large, Alice can force a win. The boundary might be where the two strategies are "balanced" and the game is a draw. Given the pattern, it seems that the threshold might be λ0 = √(2)/2? That is √2/2 ≈ 0.7071, but we saw that for λ=0.8, she lost, so that's not it. Maybe it's λ0 = 1/√2? That is 0.7071 as well. So no.

Another threshold: λ0 = 1? But she wins at 0.9. So somewhere between 0.89 and 0.9.

Maybe the threshold is where the recurrence yields a "break" at some step. Let's try to derive analytically.

We have a game where both players play max. Let's define the process precisely.

Let n be odd. After Alice's move, we have S_n = λ n. (She chooses max.) Let Q_n = some value. Then at the next even step n+1, Bazza chooses x_{n+1} = sqrt((n+1) - Q_n). Then Q_{n+1} = n+1 (since he uses max). Then S_{n+1} = S_n + sqrt((n+1) - Q_n). Then at the next odd step n+2, Alice chooses x_{n+2} = λ(n+2) - S_{n+1}, provided that is nonnegative. If it's negative, she loses. So we need to compute the sequence.

Let’s denote after odd steps, we have S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). After even steps, Q_{2k} = 2k. But note: Q_{2k} = 2k is achieved if Bazza always chooses max. However Q_{2k} is the square sum after his move. For Alice's move at odd step 2k+1, she uses S_{2k} which is the sum after the previous even turn. So we need S_{2k} as a function of λ and k.

We have recurrence:
For k≥0:
Let a_k = S_{2k} (sum after even turn 2k). And b_k = Q_{2k} = 2k (by construction).
Then at odd turn 2k+1: S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). And x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - a_k.
Then Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2 = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - a_k)^2.
Then at even turn 2k+2: Bazza chooses x_{2k+2} = sqrt((2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}) = sqrt((2k+2) - [2k + (λ(2k+1) - a_k)^2]) = sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - a_k)^2).
Then S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + x_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - a_k)^2).
So a_{k+1} = S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - a_k)^2).

Thus we have a recurrence for a_k:
a_0 = S_2 = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2). (since k=0: a_0 = S_2 = x1 + x2 = λ + sqrt(2-λ^2))
Then for k≥0:
a_{k+1} = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - a_k)^2).

Alice loses at the first odd turn when λ(2k+3) < a_{k+1}? Actually at odd turn 2k+3, she needs S_{2k+2} ≤ λ(2k+3). So if a_{k+1} > λ(2k+3), she loses. So the condition for survival is a_k ≤ λ(2k+1) for all k? Wait, for odd turn 2k+1, she needs S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). But a_k = S_{2k}. So condition: a_k ≤ λ(2k+1). That is a_k - λ(2k+1) ≤ 0. So for k=0: a_0 = λ + sqrt(2-λ^2) ≤ λ(1)? Actually 2*0+1=1, so condition: λ + sqrt(2-λ^2) ≤ λ => sqrt(2-λ^2) ≤ 0, which is impossible unless λ^2≥2? Actually for λ>√2, λ+√(2-λ^2) is not defined because √(2-λ^2) imaginary. So we have to be careful: The process only makes sense if λ ≤ √2, because otherwise x2 would be imaginary. So for λ ≤ √2, a_0 = λ + √(2-λ^2). For λ=0.9, a_0=0.9+√(2-0.81)=0.9+√1.19=0.9+1.091=1.991, and λ*1=0.9, so a_0 > λ*1, so she loses on turn 3? Wait, we earlier had for λ=0.9, she did not lose at turn 3; she survived. But here a_0 = S_2, and at turn 3, she needs S_2 ≤ λ*3 = 2.7. That's fine. But she also needs to be able to choose x_3 such that S_3 ≤ 3λ. She can choose x_3 = 3λ - S_2. If S_2 > 3λ, she loses. But S_2 is about 1.99, and 3λ=2.7, so S_2 ≤ 2.7, so she can choose x_3=0.71. So the condition for survival at turn 2k+1 is a_k ≤ λ(2k+1)? No, a_k = S_{2k}, and the bound for odd turn 2k+1 is λ(2k+1). So she needs S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). For k=0: S_0 is not defined. Actually a_0 = S_2. The odd turn after that is n=3, which is 2*1+1. So for k=1, we need S_2 ≤ λ*3. So the recurrence for a_k is defined for k≥0. The condition for survival at turn 2k+1 is a_k ≤ λ(2k+1)? Let's index: Let a_k = S_{2k} for k≥1? Actually we have a_0 = S_2 (k=0). Then odd turn 2*1+1 = 3, we need a_0 ≤ 3λ. So condition for k=0: a_0 ≤ λ*3? That's not a_k ≤ λ(2k+1) because 2k+1=1, not 3. So we need to adjust. Better to define b_k = S_{2k} for k≥1? Let's reindex.

Let's define for m≥1:
Let c_m = S_{2m} (sum after even turn 2m). For m=1: c_1 = S_2 = λ + √(2-λ^2).
Then at odd turn 2m+1, Alice chooses x_{2m+1} = λ(2m+1) - c_m, provided that c_m ≤ λ(2m+1). If not, she loses.
Then after her move, S_{2m+1} = λ(2m+1). Then Q_{2m+1} = Q_{2m} + x_{2m+1}^2 = 2m + (λ(2m+1)-c_m)^2. Then at even turn 2m+2, Bazza chooses x_{2m+2} = √((2m+2) - Q_{2m+1}) = √(2 - (λ(2m+1)-c_m)^2). Then S_{2m+2} = S_{2m+1} + x_{2m+2} = λ(2m+1) + √(2 - (λ(2m+1)-c_m)^2). So c_{m+1} = S_{2m+2} = λ(2m+1) + √(2 - (λ(2m+1)-c_m)^2).

Thus the recurrence is:
c_1 = λ + √(2-λ^2).
For m≥1:
c_{m+1} = λ(2m+1) + √(2 - (λ(2m+1) - c_m)^2.

The question is: For a given λ, can she ensure that at some odd turn, the cumulative square sum exceeds the next even bound? This is a kind of "pursuit" where she tries to push Q above the line. Bazza will try to keep Q low by choosing small x? But he is forced to keep Q ≤ n. He can choose x_n = 0 to keep Q unchanged. So he can keep Q at most the bound, but if Alice pushes Q beyond the bound, then he loses. So Alice's goal is to make Q_n > n at the start of an even turn. She can try to do that by making her moves large. But Bazza might be able to keep Q low by playing small numbers on his turns? However if Bazza plays 0, then Q stays the same, which might make it easier for Alice to push it over later? Actually if Bazza plays 0, then Q_{2k+2} = Q_{2k+1} (since x_{2k+2}=0). So if Alice makes Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, then at even turn 2k+2, Bazza cannot move because Q_{2k+2} = Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, so the bound fails even with x=0. So Alice wants to achieve Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2. She can try to do that by maximizing her moves. The maximum she can achieve at turn 2k+1 is x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}. So Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2. Since she can control S_{2k} (through her previous moves), she can try to maximize the increase. However Bazza can also control S_{2k} by his choices. So it's a sequential game.

Perhaps we can analyze using "invariants" or "potential functions". Let's try to find a condition on λ such that Alice can force a win. For λ > √2, she wins immediately. For λ ≤ √2, the immediate win is not possible. She might try to force a win by making Q exceed n+1 at some even turn. She can do that if she can achieve that after her move, Q_n > n+1. But note that at even turn n, the bound is Q_n ≤ n. So if she makes Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, then Bazza loses at turn 2k+2. So she wants to achieve Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2 for some k. Let's denote after her move at odd turn 2k+1, we have S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) (if she chooses max), and Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2. For this to be > 2k+2, we need Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2 > 2k+2. Since Q_{2k} ≤ 2k (by Bazza's previous moves, he can keep Q at most 2k because he must keep Q ≤ 2k at his turns). Actually Bazza can choose x_n = 0 to keep Q constant, but if he wants to win, he might try to make S large. However the maximum possible Q_{2k} is 2k if Bazza uses max. But if Bazza chooses smaller x, Q_{2k} could be less than 2k. So Alice might have a chance to push Q over. But Bazza will try to keep Q low by, say, playing 0. So the worst case for Alice is that Bazza always chooses x_n = 0, which gives Q_n = Q_{n-1}. Then Q_{2k} = Q_1 (since he chooses 0 each time). But then he might also try to cause her to lose? We need to consider optimal play.

We can think of this as a "two-player" game where each player chooses a number to control their own sum. This is reminiscent of a "game of sums" where the outcome is determined by a "critical" value where the players' strategies intersect. Often such problems are solved by considering that Alice wants to keep the square sum low enough that Bazza can always move? Actually she wants to make it high enough to cause Bazza to lose. So she wants to increase Q. Bazza wants to keep Q low enough to avoid losing, but he also wants to increase S to cause Alice to lose. So it's a kind of "race".

We can try to find a strategy for Bazza to win for λ small enough. For λ=0.5, we saw that if Bazza plays max, Alice loses on turn 3. So Bazza wins. For λ=0.8, the max strategy for both leads to a draw. But could Bazza force a win by deviating? For example, if at n=2, Bazza chooses x2 = 0 instead of max, then we saw Alice loses at n=5? We'll start with definitions.

Let the game proceed in turns n=1,2,3,... At odd n, Alice chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that ∑_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n. At even n, Bazza chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that ∑_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n.

If a player cannot choose a nonnegative x_n satisfying the inequality, the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither wins.

We need to determine all λ>0 for which Alice has a winning strategy and those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

We'll denote S_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i, Q_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i^2.

Observation: If λ > √2, Alice can win immediately by choosing x_1 = λ. Then Q_1 = λ^2 > 2, so even with x_2 = 0, the inequality Q_2 ≤ 2 fails (since Q_2 = Q_1 + 0 > 2). So Bazza cannot move at turn 2. Thus Alice wins. So for λ > √2, Alice wins.

The best she can do is to set x1=0, giving S2=√2, and then d_1 = 3λ - √2 (since S2=√2, then Alice's next move at turn 3: x3 = 3λ - S2 = 3λ - √2). So the initial difference she can achieve is d_1 = 3λ - √2. This is larger than the d_1 from x1=λ. So to maximize her chance, she should set x1=0. Then the recurrence for the subsequent differences becomes: d_1 = 3λ - √2; and then for k≥1, d_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - d_k^2) (the same recurrence). Now, we need to see if this orbit can exceed √2. The condition for the orbit to increase is d_1 > d_l. So we need 3λ - √2 > λ + √(1-λ^2) => 2λ > √2 + √(1-λ^2). This is the threshold we solved, giving λ^2 > (11+4√6)/25. So for λ > √((11+4√6)/25), Alice can win by playing x1=0 and then playing the max strategy on subsequent turns. For λ = √((11+4√6)/25), we have d_1 = d_l, so the orbit is constant at d_l, which is less than √2 (since for λ>1/√2, d_l < √2). Thus it's a draw. For λ between 1/√2 and that threshold, we have d_1 < d_l, so the orbit decreases and converges to d_s > 0, never exceeding √2, so it's a draw.

Now, we also need to consider the possibility that Bazza might have a winning strategy for λ > 1/√2. He cannot force a win because the stable fixed point d_s is positive, so if Alice plays max, she can always survive. Could he win by playing a different strategy? The worst-case for Alice is that Bazza tries to make the orbit go negative. But if λ > 1/√2, the stable fixed point is positive, so the orbit will converge to a positive number, not negative. However, it's possible that by playing a suboptimal move early, Bazza could cause the orbit to become negative? For example, if he chooses a smaller x2, then S2 is smaller, so d_1 = 3λ - S2 is larger, which could make it even larger, not smaller. So that would help Alice. So his best chance is to play max, which leads to the orbit we analyzed. Thus Bazza cannot force a win for λ > 1/√2. So the only case where Bazza wins is λ < 1/√2.

Now, we also need to confirm that for λ < 1/√2, Bazza indeed has a winning strategy. He can adopt the max strategy: at each even turn, he chooses x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}). This ensures that after his move, Q_n = n. We need to show that eventually Alice cannot move. Using the recurrence for d_k starting from d_1 = 2λ - √(2-λ^2). Since λ < 1/√2, d_1 is negative? Actually if λ < 1/√2, we can check: For λ=0.7, 2λ=1.4, √(2-0.49)=√1.51=1.229, so d_1=0.171>0. So d_1 is positive. But the stable fixed point d_s = λ - √(1-λ^2) is negative. Since the orbit converges to a negative number, and it starts above d_l? We need to analyze the behavior: If d_1 > d_l? d_l = λ + √(1-λ^2). For λ=0.7, d_l=0.7+0.714=1.414, so d_1=0.171<d_l, so the orbit decreases. Since d_s is negative, the orbit will eventually become negative. So there exists some k such that d_k < 0. At that point, Alice cannot choose a nonnegative x_{2k+1} because the required number is negative. Thus she loses. So Bazza wins.

Thus the classification is complete.

Now, we need to present the final answer sets.

We have:

- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > (1/5)√(11+4√6).
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if 0 < λ < 1/√2.
- For all λ with 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ (1/5)√(11+4√6), the game is a draw.

Now, we should also note that the case λ = 1/√2 is included in the draw region, and λ = (1/5)√(11+4√6) is also a draw.

Now, we can produce the proof.

Now, write the final answer.</think><solution>
We analyze the game for a positive real parameter \(\lambda\).  The moves are:

- On odd turn \(n\) Alice chooses \(x_n\ge 0\) with \(\sum_{i=1}^n x_i\le \lambda n\).
- On even turn \(n\) Bazza chooses \(x_n\ge 0\) with \(\sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2\le n\).

If a player cannot choose a suitable non‑negative number the other player wins; if the game continues forever neither wins.

Denote \(S_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i\) and \(Q_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2\).  The game is symmetric in the sense that each player tries to make it impossible for the opponent to move.

---

### 1.  Immediate win for Alice when \(\lambda>\sqrt2\)

If \(\lambda>\sqrt2\) Alice chooses \(x_1=\lambda\).  Then \(Q_1=\lambda^2>2\) and even with \(x_2=0\) the inequality \(Q_2\le2\) fails.  Bazza cannot move at turn \(2\), so Alice wins.  
Thus for \(\lambda>\sqrt2\) Alice has a winning strategy.

---

### 2.  Analysis for \(\lambda\le\sqrt2\)

From now on assume \(\lambda\le\sqrt2\).  We study the “max” strategy:  
Alice always chooses the largest allowed \(x_n\) on her turns, i.e.  
\(x_{2k+1}=\lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}\);  
Bazza always chooses the largest allowed \(x_n\) on his turns, i.e.  
\(x_{2k+2}=\sqrt{(2k+2)-Q_{2k+1}}\).

This is the natural optimal play because each player wants to hinder the opponent.

**Initial state.**  Alice’s first move: \(x_1=\lambda\) (the maximum).  
Then \(S_1=\lambda,\; Q_1=\lambda^2\).  
Bazza at turn \(2\): \(x_2=\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\);  
\(S_2=\lambda+\sqrt{2-\lambda^2},\; Q_2=2\).  
Alice at turn \(3\): \(x_3=3\lambda-S_2=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\).  Set \(d_1=x_3\).

After her move \(S_3=3\lambda,\; Q_3=2+d_1^2\).  
Bazza at turn \(4\): \(x_4=\sqrt{4-Q_3}=\sqrt{2-d_1^2}\).  
Then \(S_4=3\lambda+\sqrt{2-d_1^2},\; Q_4=4\).  
Alice at turn \(5\): \(x_5=5\lambda-S_4=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-d_1^2}\).  Set \(d_2=x_5\).

Proceeding, we obtain the recurrence

\[
d_{k+1}=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-d_k^2},\qquad k\ge1,
\]

with \(d_1=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\).  (If at some step the argument of the square root becomes negative, then Bazza cannot move at the next even turn; Alice wins.)

---

### 3.  Dynamics of the recurrence

Define \(f(d)=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-d^2}\).  For a fixed \(\lambda\), \(f\) is increasing in \(d\).  The fixed points satisfy \(d=f(d)\):

\[
\sqrt{2-d^2}=2\lambda-d\;\Longrightarrow\; d^2-2\lambda d+(2\lambda^2-1)=0,
\]

so the roots are

\[
d_s=\lambda-\sqrt{1-\lambda^2},\qquad d_l=\lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}.
\]

Note that \(d_s\le d_l\); equality occurs only when \(\lambda=1/\sqrt2\).  Also \(d_l\le\sqrt2\) with equality only at \(\lambda=1/\sqrt2\).  For \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2\) we have \(d_s<0\); for \(\lambda>1/\sqrt2\) we have \(d_s>0\).

Because \(f\) is increasing, the iteration \(d_{k+1}=f(d_k)\) is monotone:  
if \(d_k>d_l\) then \(d_{k+1}>d_k\); if \(d_k<d_l\) then \(d_{k+1}<d_k\).  
Moreover the fixed point \(d_s\) is stable (\(|f'(d_s)|<1\) when \(d_s>0\)), so the orbit converges to \(d_s\).

---

### 4.  When does Bazza win?

If \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2\) then \(d_s<0\).  The orbit converges to a negative number.  Starting from \(d_1=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\) (which is positive for small \(\lambda\), e.g. \(\lambda=0.7\) gives \(d_1>0\)), the sequence is strictly decreasing and eventually becomes negative.  Hence for some \(k\) we have \(d_k<0\); at that odd turn Alice would need to choose a negative \(x_{2k+1}\) – impossible.  Bazza wins.  
Thus for \(0<\lambda<1/\sqrt2\) Bazza has a winning strategy.

---

### 5.  When does Alice win?

Alice can influence the initial difference by her first move.  The best she can do is to set \(x_1=0\) (then \(S_2=\sqrt2\) and \(Q_2=2\)).  This gives

\[
d_1 = 3\lambda-\sqrt2.
\]

If this \(d_1\) exceeds the unstable fixed point \(d_l\), then the orbit will be increasing and eventually exceed \(\sqrt2\); when \(d_k>\sqrt2\) the next even turn is impossible because \(Q_{2k+1}=2k+d_k^2>2k+2\).  Hence Alice wins.

The condition \(3\lambda-\sqrt2 > \lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) simplifies to

\[
2\lambda > \sqrt2+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}.
\]

Solving (square twice) yields

\[
\lambda^2 > \frac{11+4\sqrt6}{25}.
\]

Therefore for \(\lambda > \frac15\sqrt{11+4\sqrt6}\) Alice can win by playing \(x_1=0\) and then using the max strategy on all subsequent turns.  (For \(\lambda\) exactly equal to \(\frac15\sqrt{11+4\sqrt6}\) we have \(d_1=d_l\) and the orbit stays at \(d_l<\sqrt2\), so it is a draw.)

---

### 6.  Draw region

For \(\lambda\) between \(1/\sqrt2\) and \(\frac15\sqrt{11+4\sqrt6}\) we have \(d_1 < d_l\) (with the max strategy starting from \(x_1=\lambda\)), so the orbit decreases and converges to \(d_s>0\).  It never becomes negative and never exceeds \(\sqrt2\).  Both players can force an infinite play by using the max strategy, so the game is a draw.  (The endpoints \(\lambda=1/\sqrt2\) and \(\lambda=\frac15\sqrt{11+4\sqrt6}\) also give draws, as shown above.)

---

### 7.  Conclusion

- Alice has a winning strategy **iff** \(\lambda > \dfrac15\sqrt{11+4\sqrt6}\).
- Bazza has a winning strategy **iff** \(0 < \lambda < \dfrac1{\sqrt2}\).
- For \(\dfrac1{\sqrt2} \le \lambda \le \dfrac15\sqrt{11+4\sqrt6}\) the game is a draw (neither player can force a win).

(Note that \(\lambda>\sqrt2\) is included in the first set because \(\frac15\sqrt{11+4\sqrt6}<\sqrt2\).)
</solution>
